<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="total-magnification-of-a-microscope">TOTAL MAGNIFICATION OF A MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#total-magnification-of-a-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the compound microscope, the magnified image is produced by two sets of lenses—the objective and the eyepiece. The objective produces a real, magnified, inverted image of the object which is brought into focus by the field lens of the eyepiece. This primary image when viewed by the eye lens is within the focal length of the lens and a magnified virtual image is produced (Fig. 3.23).</p>
<p>The total magnification of the microscope is thus the magnification of the objective multiplied by that of the eyepiece. For example, using a 40X objective and a 10X eyepiece, the object will be magnified 400 times. If a 100X objective is used with a 10X eyepiece, it will give an increased magnification of 1,000X. When this increased magnification results in bringing further details of the object into view, it is called useful magnification. If the enlargement of the object fails to bring into view any more detail, and looses the sharpness of the image, it is called empty magnification (Fig. 3.19b).</p>
<p>Resolving power of the microscope plays a major role in increasing the useful magnification of the microscope. It should be noted that because of the combined action of both the lens systems of the compound microscope, the objective and the ocular, the final image seen is upside down and reversed. The left side appears as the right, and the top as the bottom, and vice versa. This should be borne in mind while moving the object under observation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="use-and-care-of-a-bright-field-microscope">USE AND CARE OF A BRIGHT-FIELD MICROSCOPE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#use-and-care-of-a-bright-field-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A microscope is a very delicate and expensive instrument. It must, therefore, be handled with care. The following points are to be borne in mind while using the microscope.</p>
<ol>
<li>Always lift and carry the microscope well supported with hands.</li>
<li>Protect the microscope from dust, moisture and direct sunlight.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-02b85dd1.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg">
Fig. 3.23 Formation of inverted, virtual, magnified image in the light microscope</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Place the microscope on a firm surface so that it does not vibrate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The user should be seated at the correct height for the convenient use of the microscope.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The user must be seated away from direct sunlight.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use the flat side of the mirror if the microscope has no built-in source of illumination.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The underside of a glass slide should be completely dry before it is placed onto the stage.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The specimen should be viewed with or without oil depending on the type of objective employed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is always advisable to start viewing with the low power objective (10X).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Look at the objective from the side when lowering it on the object. Then look into the eyepiece and raise slowly to focus.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Note</strong> (a) The working distance for the low power objective is 16 mm, for the dry high power objective is about 4 mm and for the oil immersion objective is about 2 mm.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 39</p>
<p>(b) When using the low power objective, the amount of light entering the lens should be reduced by lowering the condenser and partially closing the iris diaphragm.
(c) For the high power objectives, the condenser should be raised and the iris diaphragm opened to allow in the required amount of light.</p>
<ol start="11">
<li>If the objective lens is smeared or dirty, it should be cleaned with a clean piece of soft linen or lens tissue. Little xylene may be used if the dirt is difficult to remove. Never use alcohol because it can dissolve the cement used to keep the lenses together.</li>
<li>While simple cleaning of the microscope can be undertaken by an experienced user, major cleaning or maintenance should be done by trained personnel.</li>
<li>At the end of the working day, all the objective lenses must be wiped clean and the microscope covered with its protective cover.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="microscopy">MICROSCOPY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Microscopy is the use of microscopes in all their various forms. Although the <strong>bright-field</strong> microscopy is most commonly used, the basic principle of light microscopy can be modified to perform different functions, for example, <strong>dark-field, fluorescence, phase-contrast</strong> or <strong>electron microscopy</strong>. Each of these techniques is capable of performing the basic function of the microscope, that is, magnification. In addition, they have specialised functions such as observing biochemical processes as they occur in a living cell, or a several hundred-fold increase in resolution by the use of electrons instead of light waves. Table 3.2 shows the features of different types of microscopy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bright-field-microscopy">Bright-Field Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bright-field-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bright field microscopy, as the name suggests, uses a direct source of light which illuminates the object to make it visible through the microscope. However, this method has some limitations be</p>
<p>Table 3.2 Characteristics of different types of microscopy</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type of microscopy</th><th>Total magnification</th><th>Appearance of specimen</th><th>Applications</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Bright-field</td><td>1000-2000</td><td>Specimens stained or unstained; bacteria generally stained and appear colour of stain</td><td>Gross morphological features of bacteria, yeasts, molds, algae and protozoa</td></tr><tr><td>Dark-field</td><td>1000-2000</td><td>Generally unstained; appears bright or “lighted” in an otherwise dark field</td><td>Microorganism that exhibit some characteristic morphological feature in the living state and in fluid suspension, e.g. spirochaetes</td></tr><tr><td>Fluorescence</td><td>1000-2000</td><td>Bright coloured; colour of the fluorescent dye</td><td>Diagnostic techniques where fluorescent dye fixed, to organism reveals the organism’s identity</td></tr><tr><td>Phase-contrast</td><td>1000-2000</td><td>Varying degrees of light intensity</td><td>Examination of cellular structures in living cells of the larger microorganisms e.g. yeasts, algae, protozoa and some bacteria</td></tr><tr><td>Electron</td><td>300,000-500,000</td><td>Viewed on fluorescent screen</td><td>Examination of viruses and the ultrastructure of cells</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>40 General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p>cause many objects, such as microorganisms or tissue cells, are transparent, they give a very poor contrast with a bright source of light and are not clearly visible unless they are stained with a coloured dye. Since most staining techniques lead to death of living cells or organisms, it is not possible to observe the objects in a living state.</p>
<p>The principle, working and use of the bright field microscopy has been described in detail in previous pages.</p>
<p><strong>Inverted microscope</strong> This is a modified bright-field microscope in which the lamp and the condenser are above the stage. The objective, body and eyepieces are below the stage. The eyepieces are inclined upwards for convenience. The inverted microscope is useful for examination of cell lines, viral cultures and for urine microscopy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dark-field-microscopy">Dark-Field Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dark-field-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This method is valuable in the examination of unstained living organisms. For example the causative agent of syphilis, <em>Treponema pallidum</em>, can be identified in the specimen under dark-field microscopy by its characteristic spiral shape and motility. The dark field microscopy uses a light microscope equipped with a special condenser and objective which brightly illuminate the objects against a dark background. The object can be compared with the moon shining brightly in the dark night sky because it reflects the sun rays to the earth and appears as a self-illuminated star. The same principle is also observed when the dust particles floating in the air in a dark room appear bright in a stream of sunlight.</p>
<p><strong>Principle</strong> Dark-field microscopy, also known as dark-ground illumination, makes use of a special condenser which has a central blacked-out area. Therefore, the light coming from the source cannot directly enter into the objective and the microscope field appears dark. The path of light is directed in such a way that it can pass through the outer edge of the condenser at a wide angle (Fig. 3.24). In its path, the light ray may strike the object in the object plane which will reflect the light. This reflected light then passes through the objective, making the object appear to be luminiscent against a dark background.</p>
<p><strong>Setting up dark-field microscope</strong> The special dark-field condenser produces the best form of dark-ground illumination. It is very useful when a high magnification is required. Ordinarily, adequate dark-ground can be obtained with <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>40</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">40X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">40</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> objectives using a simple opaque disc which is inserted into the filter holder of the substage to prevent light passing through the condenser to the objective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="requirements">Requirements<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#requirements" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>An oil immersion or dry dark-ground condenser with centering screws.</li>
<li>A funnel stop for insertion in the <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>100</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">100X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">100</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> objective to reduce its numerical aperture to less than one.</li>
<li>A <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>6</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">6X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">6</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> or <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> eyepiece. The lower the power of the eyepiece, the brighter will be the image.</li>
<li>A high intensity microscope lamp, if the microscope is not provided with a built-in source.</li>
<li>Good quality slides, not more than one mm thick, free from scratches and absolutely clean.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="setting-up">Setting up<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#setting-up" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Replace the ordinary condenser with the dark-ground condenser and raise to almost stage level.</li>
<li>Using a thoroughly clean slide and cover slip, make a thin wet preparation of the object.</li>
<li>Add a drop of oil on top of the condenser and place the slide on the stage so that the oil is between the slide and the condenser.</li>
<li>Place a high intensity lamp about 18 inches away. Adjust the mirror so that light passes upwards into the condenser.</li>
<li>With the <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mi>X</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10X</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07847em;">X</span></span></span></span></span> objective, focus on the specimen until a small ring of light is seen illuminating a part of the specimen.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 41</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c9a398f9.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg">
Fig. 3.24 Dark ground illumination</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Focus the condenser up or down until the small ring of light becomes just a spot of light.</li>
<li>Using the centering screws, bring the spot of light to the centre of the field.</li>
<li>Focus with the 40X objective and examine. Further adjustments of the centering screws and focussing of the condenser can be made.</li>
<li>The oil immersion objective can be used after inserting a funnel stop.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="some-examples-of-application-of-dark-ground-microscopy">Some examples of application of dark-ground microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-examples-of-application-of-dark-ground-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Detection of <em>Treponema pallidum</em> in chancre fluid, the motility of which cannot be seen in ordinary light microscope.</li>
<li>Detection of <em>Leptospira</em> in urine.</li>
<li>Detection of <em>Borrelia</em> in blood.</li>
<li>Examination of stool for the presence of <em>Vibrio cholerae</em> and <em>Campylobacter</em> species.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="problems-in-the-use-of-dark-ground-microscope">Problems in the use of dark-ground microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#problems-in-the-use-of-dark-ground-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Problems are frequently encountered in the use of dark-ground microscopes. These include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Difficulty in focussing or centring of the dark-ground condenser.</li>
<li>Using slides and coverslips that are not completely clean.</li>
<li>Examining too dense a preparation.</li>
<li>The presence of air bubble in the immersion oil.</li>
<li>Insufficient oil contact between the slide and the condenser.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fluorescence-microscopy">Fluorescence Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fluorescence-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A substance is said to be fluorescent when it absorbs the energy of short light waves, such as blue</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p>light, and releases or emits the light of a longer wavelength, such as green or red light. This phenomenon is called fluorescence. It is being increasingly used in clinical laboratories because it can be adapted to rapid tests for the detection of microorganisms, antibodies and many other substances. When fluorescence microscopy is used for the detection of antigen-antibody reaction, it is known as immunofluorescence.</p>
<p>Principle In fluorescence microscopy, the "invisible" ultraviolet light is used to "illuminate" particles or microorganisms which have been previously treated with fluorescing dyes known as fluorochromes. The fluorescent dye absorbs the invisible UV light of shorter wavelength and emits light of longer wavelength which is within the visible spectrum of light. Thus the background is dark because there is no visible light, and only the specimen (cells, microorganisms, etc.) stained with a</p>
<p>fluorescent dye appears as a bright object. An example of a commonly used fluorescent dye in immunofluorescence is fluorescein isothiocyanate (FIT) which gives green fluorescence.</p>
<p>The light path of ultraviolet rays first passes through an optical filter, called exciter filter, which removes other non-specific wavelengths (colours) of light and allows only the UV rays. Using an immersion dark-ground condenser, the light is directed obliquely onto the object. These rays then pass through the specimen stained with a fluorochrome. The fluorochrome will absorb the UV light and emit visible light, making the object visible in its characteristic colour. A yellow barrier filter is fixed just above the objectives to prevent dangerous UV rays from entering the eye of the observer. A recent development in fluorescence microscopy is the use of reflected light and dichroic filters which has resulted in better illumination. Figure 3.25 shows the essential parts of a fluorescence microscope.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-acfc69ca.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg">
Fig. 3.25 Essential parts of a fluorescence microscope</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 43</p>
<p>Setting up fluorescence microscope Fluorescence microscope is available as a complete unit and can be set up using manufacturer's instructions. However, an ordinary light microscope can be converted into a fluorescence microscope with some accessories as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>Set up the expensive mercury vapour lamp (which is very bulky and clumsy to use) or relatively smaller and cheaper quartz iodine lamp in front of the mirror.</li>
<li>Fix the dark ground condenser in its place; and focus and centre the condenser with the aid of a non-fluorescing immersion fluid such as liquid paraffin.</li>
<li>Insert the primary exciter filter in the filter holder in the substage condenser.</li>
<li>Insert the barrier filter above the objective, but below the eyepiece.</li>
<li>Place the specimen stained with the appropriate fluorochrome on the stage and examine. A successful fluorescence microscopy very much depends on experience.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="some-applications-of-fluorescence-microscopy">Some applications of fluorescence microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-applications-of-fluorescence-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Detection of acid-fast bacilli (AFB) in sputum or cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) when stained with auramine fluorescent dye.</li>
<li>Examination of acridine-orange stained specimens for the detection of <em>Trichomonas vaginalis</em>, intracellular gonococci and meningococci, and other parasites and microorganisms.</li>
<li>In immunodiagnosis, using both direct and indirect antibody techniques. It is very useful with monoclonal reagents because of the high specificity.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phase-contrast-microscopy">Phase Contrast Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phase-contrast-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An advantage of this technique is its ability to show cell structure without using dyes or killing the cell or the organism. Thus it is possible to study life processes <em>in situ</em>.</p>
<p>The phase contrast microscopy uses a modified light microscope that enhances the contrast between substances of different thickness or different density. In bright field microscopy, the unstained objects in the specimen are visible because of the change in the amplitude of the light ray passing through them producing a contrast with the surrounding medium. When a ray of light passes through a part of the specimen having a lower density, a very small amount of light is absorbed and the emerging light wave will have a comparatively higher amplitude or intensity. Another light ray, passing through a denser part of the specimen, will emerge with a lower amplitude or intensity due to higher absorption by the dense specimen. This part of the specimen, therefore, will appear darker. However, these differences in intensity are very subtle and the human eye is not sensitive enough to notice the minute differences. The purpose of phase contrast microscope is to convert optical densities or refractive indices into amplitude changes which are easily visible to the eye.</p>
<p><strong>Principle</strong> A ray of light is made of waves travelling in a straight line. When two such waves travel together, they are said to be “in phase”. Such a ray will appear bright to the observer. However, if one of the waves is held up or made to change the path, they will no longer travel together and they are said to “interfere” with each other, differing in the intensity.</p>
<p>In a phase-contrast microscope, a special condenser and objective control the illumination in a way that accentuates the differences in densities. It causes light to travel different routes through the various parts of the cell. The result is an image with differing degrees of darkness and brightness, collectively called “contrast”.</p>
<p>To achieve phase contrast, one needs:</p>
<ol>
<li>An annular diaphragm (annulus), appropriate for the objective.</li>
<li>A phase plate</li>
<li>A high intensity compound lamp</li>
<li>An auxiliary telescope</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p>The annulus is placed in the condenser and the phase plate is placed in the objective. The annulus allows only a small ring of light to pass into the microscope. The size of annulus required varies with the numerical aperture of the objective used. A low power objective requires the annulus of a smaller diameter than that for the high power objective. The phase plate has a circle engraved on it. This circle matches with the ring of light coming in from the annulus through the condenser.</p>
<p>The direct rays of light coming in through the annulus thus pass through the engraved circle and take the shortest route to the observer by 1/4th of a wavelength. However, some light rays are reflected by the object, the reflection being proportional to the density. These rays are diverted from the shortest route and interfere with the direct waves, producing variation in the intensity of illumination. This results in the increased contrast in the observed image. With this method, denser materials appear bright, while parts of the cell that have a density close to that of water (e.g., the cytoplasm) appear dark. Figure 3.26 shows the light path of a phase contrast microscope.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="setting-up-phase-contrast-microscope">Setting up phase-contrast microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#setting-up-phase-contrast-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>With a high intensity lamp, illuminate the microscope. Turn the required objective into position and focus on the specimen.</li>
<li>Move the annulus matching with the objective into place.</li>
<li>Remove the eyepiece and insert the telescope; adjust until the two rings, one bright and one dark, are in focus.</li>
<li>Adjust the centring screws of the condenser until the bright ring of the annulus fits exactly into the darker ring of the phase plate.</li>
<li>Remove the telescope, replace the eyepiece, focus and examine the specimen.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="some-applications-of-phase-contrast-microscopy">Some applications of phase contrast microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-applications-of-phase-contrast-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>For examining unstained bacteria e.g. vibrios in specimens.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-9f35ced2.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg">
Fig. 3.26 Light path of phase contrast</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>For examining wet preparations of specimens like urine, vaginal swabs.</li>
<li>For examining faecal preparations for trophozoites of amoebae.</li>
<li>In searching for trypanosomes in blood and other body fluids.</li>
<li>In the detection of promastigotes of Leishmania in culture fluid.</li>
</ol>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 45</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="interference-microscopy">Interference Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interference-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The basic difference between interference microscopy and phase contrast microscopy is that the former does not rely on the diffraction of light by the object, but generates mutually interfering beams which produce contrast. It is this feature that enables the microscope to accurately measure the phase change in an object as small as 1/300th of a wavelength. In addition, this technique gives the viewer a three dimensional image of the object under study.</p>
<p>For interference microscopy, the brightfield microscope is modified by the addition of a special beam-splitting (Wollaston) prism to the condenser. When a beam of light is split by the prism, one passes through the specimen, which alters the amplitude of the wave; while the other does not pass through the specimen and serves as a reference beam. These two dissimilar beams then pass through the objective and are recombined by a second beam-combining (Wollaston) prism. This recombination of light waves gives a three-dimensional image (Fig 3.27).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b9c467e7.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg">
Fig. 3.27 Light path of interference microscope</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="some-applications-of-interference-microscopy">Some applications of interference microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-applications-of-interference-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>To study individual parts of living cells with maximum resolution of detail.</li>
<li>To estimate dry mass when it is applied as a highly accurate optical balance.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="polarizing-microscope">Polarizing Microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#polarizing-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A polarizer, or a polarizing filter, may be imagined as a sieve which filters ordinary light waves (which vibrate in all directions) and allows only light waves of one orientation (e.g., north-south) to pass through the filter. Thus polarized light vibrates only in one plane. Some crystalline substances are able to filter out all other planes of vibration from ordinary light except the one which lies on the optical axis of the substance. One such substance is the Icelandic spar. This can be cut and mounted to form structures known as Nicol prisms.</p>
<p>In a polarizing microscope, a polarizing filter, such as Nicol prism, is placed between the light source and the specimen. The second polarizer, called analyser, is placed above the specimen between the objective and the eyepiece. One of the polarizers is then rotated until the two are at right angle to each other. This will be seen as absence of light through the eyepiece because the two polarized light waves will cancel each other (e.g. north-south and east-west waves). However, certain objects have a property called birefringence, which means that they rotate or polarize light. The objects that can polarize light can be seen with this system and will appear bright against a dark background.</p>
<p>In recent years, other polaroid material has replaced Icelandic spar. Although it does not give as dark a background as the latter, it is just adequate for most purposes.</p>
<p>The polarizing microscope is useful in a clinical laboratory for the study of synovial fluid and urinary sediment, and in some histological work; and sometimes in forensic medicine. It is also used for the detection of the presence of silica, asbestos, mica and talc in granulomas, and in the identifica</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>46 General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p>tion of hooklets of certain parasites. This technique is more commonly used in geology for particle analysis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="projection-microscopy">Projection Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#projection-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is used for the projection of enlarged images of tissue sections on a microscope glass slide. The slide is projected onto a suitable screen for visual aid or demonstration.</p>
<p>There are two types of projection microscopes:</p>
<ol>
<li>The microprojector, suitable for a limited audience. It has reduced light intensity and therefore, poor resolution.</li>
<li>The other large model gives a large, bright image over long distances. However, the slides tend to fade with prolonged projection.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="electron-microscopy">Electron Microscopy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electron-microscopy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The maximum resolution obtained with a microscope that uses light waves (wavelength approximately 300 to 700 nm) to illuminate the object is limited to 0.2 microns or 200 nm. Therefore, most viruses and other very fine structures cannot be seen with any light microscope. However, if a beam of electrons, having a wavelength of 0.05 nm is used, the resolution can increase dramatically by about 2000 times (0.1 nm).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="working-principle-of-an-electron-microscope">Working principle of an electron microscope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#working-principle-of-an-electron-microscope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The convergence of a light beam by a convex lens has its counterparts in the convergence of an electron beam as it passes through the core of a circular magnetic field. Most electron microscopes use electromagnetic lenses. The conveyance of an electron beam produces an image. The image and object distances are related to the focal length of the lens in exactly the same way as in light optics. The electron microscope is therefore constructed on the same optical principles as the light microscope and the same formulae can be used to correlate magnification and focal distances.</p>
<p>The electron beam is obtained from a heated tungsten filament which is surrounded by a metal cylinder known as the Whenalt cap. This cap serves to shape the electron beam. Just beyond the Whenalt cap is the anode which has an aperture through which the electron beam passes. A large voltage is applied between the cathode (the tungsten filament) and the anode, this gives the electrons their high velocity. The wavelength of electron is inversely proportional to their velocity, and therefore they have a very short wavelength (0.1–0.5Å) or 0.01–0.05 nm. The electrons pass through the rest of the microscope without any further acceleration.</p>
<p>The electron beam first passes through the condenser lens. As in the light microscope, this lens serves to focus the beam to the object, and so provides illumination. The lenses in the electron microscope consist of circular electromagnetic fields. The magnetic lens of an electron microscope can have different powers (focal lengths and magnification) depending on the amount of current flowing through the electrical coils. In the light microscope, the lens powers are fixed, but the lenses are movable with respect to the object so that the image can be focussed and proper conditions of illumination obtained. In the electron microscope, all the lenses are rigidly fixed, but their focal points are variable by changing lens currents. Thus, the illumination of the object is achieved by varying the current in the condenser lens.</p>
<p>The imaging system of the electron microscope usually consists of two lenses—the objective and the projector lens. A third, intermediate lens, may be present between the two. This gives three stages of magnification and makes it possible to achieve high magnification in a reasonable amount of space. However, the numerical aperture of an electron microscope lens is very small and the best resolution is about 4 Å i.e., 0.3 – 0.5 nm (about 500 times better than that of light microscope which is 0.2 μm).</p>
<p>The objective lens is placed with its focal point close to the object. Intermediate images are formed between each lens system. The projector throws its image on to a fluorescent screen (Fig. 3.28a) which</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 47</p>
<p>may be substituted by a photographic plate to make a permanent record.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-1d95e563.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg">
Fig. 3.28 (a) Transmission electron microscope</p>
<p>The entire illuminating imaging system is usually referred to as the microscope column and is constructed upside down compared to light microscope. The electron and the condenser are placed above and the image formed below. The column is very rigidly constructed and is maintained in high vacuum since air molecules would deflect the electron beam. The specimen is introduced into the object plane through an air lock. Therefore, it is not possible to examine living material in the electron microscope.</p>
<p>Electron optics are essentially similar to light optics. One important difference, however, is that the formation of the image is due to scattering of electrons by the molecules of the specimen. This scattering solely depends on mass densities. Elements of high atomic weight such as lead or uranium cause marked electron scatter and appear very dense in the electron image. The lighter elements such as carbon, oxygen and nitrogen cause less electron scatter and have poor contrast. In the electron microscope, the image is due to absorption of light which depends more on molecular structure than atomic weights.</p>
<p>Most biological stains depend on the absorption of certain wavelengths of light due to their molecular structure and are composed mainly of carbon, nitrogen and hydrogen atoms. Since these are all of low atomic weight they have little electron scattering power and are not generally useful as stains for electron microscopy. Unstained tissues have very poor contrast in electron microscopy. They may be stained by a variety of heavy metal salts. Most such 'stains' are relatively non-specific, e.g., lead citrate, lead hydroxide and uranyl acetate. The sections are supported on thin plastic grids.</p>
<p>A disadvantage of the electron microscopy is that it is necessary to examine the objects in vacuum because the gas molecules in the air can scatter electrons and distort the image. Therefore only dead and dried objects can be examined in the electron microscope, which may lead to considerable distortion in the object. There are several techniques available for this microscope, including staining methods with heavy metals and radioactive substances. The method used depends on the type of electron microscope and the purpose of the examination.</p>
<p>In transmission electron microscopy, heavy metals can be used as stains, making some parts of the cell appear dark because electrons cannot penetrate them.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>48 General Approach to Medical Laboratory Science</p>
<p>With the scanning electron microscopy (Fig. 3.28b), a three-dimensional view of the object can be obtained. The object is coated with a thin film of metal. The beam of electrons moves back and forth over the surface of the object to produce a pattern which depicts the structure of the object. This pattern can be detected by a device similar to a television camera.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c896e80d.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg">
Fig. 3.28(b) Scanning electron microscope</p>
<p>For a more detailed information on electron microscopy, refer to the Section on Histopathological Techniques.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="micrometry">MICROMETRY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#micrometry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Micrometry is the measurement of microscopic objects using a calibrated eyepiece micrometer. The unit of measurement is the micron (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>μ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mu</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span></span></span></span></span> or <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>μ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mu</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span></span></span></span></span>m). The eyepiece micrometer scale requires calibration from a measured scale engraved on a micrometer glass slide (graticule).</p>
<p>The eyepiece micrometer is calibrated for each microscope objective at which the measurement is required. For example, for parasitology, the scale should be calibrated to 40X objective while for haematology, it should be 100X objective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="method-of-calibrating-an-eyepiece-micrometer">Method of Calibrating an Eyepiece Micrometer<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#method-of-calibrating-an-eyepiece-micrometer" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Unscrew the upper lens of a 10X huygenian eyepiece and insert the eyepiece micrometer disc, with engraved side facing down.</li>
<li>Place the stage graticule slide on the stage; rotate the required objective into position and bring the scale into focus. Both the eyepiece scale and the calibration scale should be focused clearly.</li>
<li>Adjust the field until 0 line of the eyepiece scale aligns exactly with the 0 line of the calibration scale.</li>
<li>Look along the scales and note where a division of the eyepiece scale exactly aligns with a division of the calibration scale.</li>
<li>Measure the distance between the 0 point and where the alignment occurs on the graticule. The measurement of the calibration scale are 0.1 mm to 2.0 mm. Each small subdivision measures 0.01 mm.</li>
<li>Count the number of divisions of the eyepiece scale between the 0 point and where the alignment occurs.</li>
<li>Calculate the measurement for 1 division of the eyepiece scale in microns (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>μ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mu</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.625em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal">μ</span></span></span></span></span>m).</li>
</ol>
<p>Figure 3.29 shows calibration of eyepiece micrometer.</p>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>
<p>General Laboratory Instruments and Equipments 49</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-efb4c20a.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg">
Fig. 3.29 Calibration of eyepiece micrometer</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example">Example<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Distance measured = 0.4 mm
Number of divisions = 25
1 division measures 0.4/25 = 0.016 mm
To convert mm to μm
0.016 × 1000 = 16 microns.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="to-measure-an-object">To measure an object<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#to-measure-an-object" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Focus the eyepiece micrometer scale and the object sharply.</li>
<li>Position the object along the scale and count the divisions covered.</li>
<li>Refer to the previously prepared table for the objective in use to obtain the measurement for the number of divisions counted. This will give the size of the object in microns.</li>
</ol>
<p>For example, an object covering 2 divisions on the scale will measure <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>16</mn><mo>×</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>32</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">16 \times 2 = 32</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">16</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">×</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">32</span></span></span></span></span> microns. Table 3.3 shows the relationship between units used for the measurement of microorganisms.</p>
<p>Table 3.3 Relationship between units used for the measurement of microorganisms</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Unit of length</th><th>Millimeter (mm)</th><th>Micrometer (μm)</th><th>Nanometer (nm)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Millimeter (mm)</td><td>1</td><td>1000 (10^{3})</td><td>1000,000 (10^{6})</td></tr><tr><td>Micrometer (μm)</td><td>0.001 (10^{-3})</td><td>1</td><td>1000 (10^{3})</td></tr><tr><td>Nanometer (nm)</td><td>0.000001 (10^{-6})</td><td>0.001 (10^{-3})</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>Angstrom (Å)</td><td>0.0000001 (10^{-7})</td><td>0.0001 (10^{-4})</td><td>0.1 (10^{-1})</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Scanned by CamScanner</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="basic-chemistry-and-laboratory-calculations">Basic Chemistry and Laboratory Calculations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#basic-chemistry-and-laboratory-calculations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A knowledge of terms used in basic chemistry is essential for full understanding of chemical reactions in Clinical Chemistry.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definitions">DEFINITIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definitions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Atom</strong> Atoms are the smallest particles of which any substance is made of that take part in a chemical reaction. An atom is composed of central compact nucleus. This nucleus contains two types of particles, protons and neutrons. The protons are positively charged while neutrons have no electrical charge. The whole nucleus is surrounded by negatively charged particles called electrons. An atom is said to be electrically neutral because the number of protons is equal to the that of electrons. Atomic number of an element is equal to the number of protons. Atomic weight of an element is the sum of the number of protons and neutrons. Isotopes are the atoms of the same element which have the same number of protons, but a different number of neutrons.</p>
<p><strong>Molecule</strong> It is the smallest particle of an element or compound that can exist independently. For example, one atom of sodium (Na⁺) and one atom of chloride (Cl⁻) combine to form one molecule of sodium chloride (NaCl).</p>
<p><strong>Molecular weight</strong> Molecular weight (or relative molecular mass) is the sum of all atomic weights of which the molecule is composed. For example, molecular weight of NaCl is 58.5 where atomic weight of Na is 23 and that of Cl is 35.5.</p>
<p><strong>Valence</strong> The valence of a substance is the number of hydrogen atoms which will combine with, liberate or replace one atom of the substance. For example, the valence of oxygen in water (H₂O) is 2.</p>
<p><strong>Equivalent weight</strong> The equivalent weight is the weight that combines with, liberates or replaces one atom of hydrogen. This is equal to molecular weight divided by the valence.</p>
<p>i.e.,</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mfrac><mtext>Molecular&nbsp;Weight</mtext><mtext>Valence</mtext></mfrac></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\frac{\text{Molecular Weight}}{\text{Valence}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.0574em; vertical-align: -0.686em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3714em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">Valence</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">Molecular&nbsp;Weight</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p><strong>Electrolyte</strong> An electrolyte is a compound which when dissolved in water will conduct electric current. The passage of electric current through an electrolyte is called electrolysis which results in the dissociation of molecules into positively charged and negatively charged ions known respectively as cations and anions.</p>
<p><strong>Oxidation</strong> This is a reaction in which an atom loses electrons. It is in effect, the addition of oxygen or removal of hydrogen from a substance.</p>

<p>Basic Chemistry and Laboratory Calculations 51</p>
<p><strong>Reduction</strong> This is a reaction in which oxygen is removed or hydrogen added to the substance; that is, an atom gains electrons.</p>
<p><strong>Radicles</strong> These are units or groups of atoms which when combined in a compound behave as a single atom. vertical-align: -0.247em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">NH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8446em;"><span style="top: -2.453em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">4</span></span></span><span style="top: -3.1362em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mbin mtight">+</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.247em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>.</p>
<p><strong>Acid</strong> It is a substance or compound that gives off hydrogen ion (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mo>+</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{H}^+</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7713em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7713em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mbin mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>) in solution and can donate a proton.</p>
<p><strong>Base</strong> It is a substance or compound which gives off hydroxyl ions (<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mo>−</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{OH}^-</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8446em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">OH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8446em;"><span style="top: -3.1362em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mbin mtight">−</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>) in solution and can accept a proton.</p>
<p>type of ion over the other determines the degree of acidity or alkalinity. It is usual to refer to this acidity or alkalinity as hydrogen ion concentration. Pure water contains <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>0.0000001</mn><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">0.0000001\ \mathrm{g}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8389em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">0.0000001</span><span class="mspace">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">g</span></span></span></span></span> or <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>7</mn></mrow></msup><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{-7}\ \mathrm{g}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.0085em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">−</span><span class="mord mtight">7</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">g</span></span></span></span></span> of hydrogen ions per litre at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>25</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">25^{\circ}\mathrm{C}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; The concentration of hydrogen ions of water is expressed as</p>
